background-color: rgb(235,235,210)"><a href="/wiki/Suborder" class="mw-redirect" title="Suborder">Suborders</a>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="text-align: left">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_wren" title="New Zealand wren">Acanthisitti</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tyranni" title="Tyranni">Tyranni</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Songbird" title="Songbird">Passeri</a></li></ul>\n<p>and see <a href="#Suborder_Acanthisitti">text</a>\n</p>\n</td></tr>\n\n\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align: center; background-color: rgb(235,235,210)"><a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">Diversity</a>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="text-align: center"><a href="#Taxonomic_list_of_Passeriformes_families">Roughly 100 families, 6,409 species</a>\n</td></tr>\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n</tbody></table>\n<p>A <b>passerine</b> is any <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">bird</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> <b>Passeriformes</b>, which includes more than half of all bird species. Sometimes known as <b>perching birds</b> or \xe2\x80\x93 less accurately \xe2\x80\x93 as <a href="/wiki/Songbird" title="Songbird">songbirds</a>, passerines are distinguished from other orders of birds by <a href="/wiki/Dactyly#In_birds" title="Dactyly">the arrangement of their toes</a> (three pointing forward and one back), which facilitates <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/perching" class="extiw" title="wikt:perching">perching</a>. With more than 110 families and some 6,409 identified species,<sup id="cite_ref-a1_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-a1-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Passeriformes is the largest order of birds and among the most diverse orders of terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The passerines contain several groups of <a href="/wiki/Brood_parasite" title="Brood parasite">brood parasites</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Viduidae" title="Viduidae">viduas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuckoo-finch" title="Cuckoo-finch">cuckoo-finches</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Cowbird" title="Cowbird">cowbirds</a>. Most passerines are <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">omnivorous</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Shrike" title="Shrike">shrikes</a> are <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">carnivorous</a>.\n</p><p>The terms "passerine" and "Passeriformes" are derived from <i>Passer domesticus</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">scientific name</a> of the eponymous species (the <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">house sparrow</a>) and ultimately from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> term <i>passer</i>, which refers to sparrows and similar small birds.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" href="#Taxonomic_list_of_Passeriformes_families"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Taxonomic list of Passeriformes families</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Arrangement_of_families"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Arrangement of families</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Suborder_Acanthisitti"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Suborder Acanthisitti</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Suborder_Tyranni_(Suboscines)"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">Suborder Tyranni (Suboscines)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Suborder_Passeri_(songbirds_or_oscines)"><span class="tocnumber">6.4</span> <span class="toctext">Suborder Passeri (songbirds or oscines)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Phylogeny"><span class="tocnumber">6.5</span> <span class="toctext">Phylogeny</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Footnotes"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Footnotes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Passerine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Passerine&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <small><i>(April 2018)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_brood_parasitic_passerines" title="List of brood parasitic passerines">List of brood parasitic passerines</a></div>\n<p>The order is divided into three suborders, <a href="/wiki/Tyranni" title="Tyranni">Tyranni</a> (suboscines), <a href="/wiki/Passeri" class="mw-redirect" title="Passeri">Passeri</a> (oscines), and the <a href="/wiki/Basal_(evolution)" class="mw-redirect" title="Basal (evolution)">basal</a> <a href="/wiki/Acanthisitti" class="mw-redirect" title="Acanthisitti">Acanthisitti</a><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>. Oscines have the best control of their <a href="/wiki/Syrinx_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrinx (biology)">syrinx</a> muscles among birds, producing a wide range of <a href="/wiki/Birdsong" class="mw-redirect" title="Birdsong">songs</a> and other vocalizations (though some of them, such as the <a href="/wiki/Crow" title="Crow">crows</a>, do not sound musical to human beings); some such as the <a href="/wiki/Lyrebird" title="Lyrebird">lyrebird</a> are accomplished imitators. The acanthisittids or <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_wren" title="New Zealand wren">New Zealand wrens</a> are tiny birds restricted to <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, at least in modern times; they were long placed in Passeri; their taxonomic position is uncertain, although they seem to be a distinct and very ancient group.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PasserinePterylosis.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/PasserinePterylosis.jpg/220px-PasserinePterylosis.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/PasserinePterylosis.jpg/330px-PasserinePterylosis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/PasserinePterylosis.jpg/440px-PasserinePterylosis.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1642" data-file-height="1156" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PasserinePterylosis.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pterylosis or the feather tracts in a typical passerine</div></div></div>\n<p>Most passerines are smaller than typical members of other avian orders. The heaviest and altogether largest passerines are the <a href="/wiki/Thick-billed_raven" title="Thick-billed raven">thick-billed raven</a> and the larger races of <a href="/wiki/Common_raven" title="Common raven">common raven</a>, each exceeding 1.5&#160;kg (3.3&#160;lb) and 70&#160;cm (28&#160;in). The <a href="/wiki/Superb_lyrebird" title="Superb lyrebird">superb lyrebird</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Bird-of-paradise" title="Bird-of-paradise">birds-of-paradise</a>, due to very long tails or tail coverts, are longer overall. The smallest passerine is the <a href="/wiki/Short-tailed_pygmy_tyrant" title="Short-tailed pygmy tyrant">short-tailed pygmy tyrant</a>, at 6.5&#160;cm (2.6&#160;in) and 4.2&#160;g (0.15&#160;oz).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy">Anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Passerine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The foot of a passerine has three toes directed forward and one toe directed backward, called <a href="/wiki/Anisodactyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Anisodactyl">anisodactyl</a> arrangement. This arrangement enables the passerine birds to perch upon vertical surfaces, such as trees and cliffs. The toes have no webbing or joining, but in some <a href="/wiki/Cotinga" title="Cotinga">cotingas</a>, the second and third toes are united at their basal third. The hind toe joins the leg at the same level as the front toes. The passeriformes have this toe arrangement in common with hunting birds like eagles and falcons.\n</p><p>The leg arrangement of passerine birds contains a special adaptation for perching. A tendon in the rear of the leg running from the underside of the toes to the muscle behind the <a href="/wiki/Tibiotarsus" title="Tibiotarsus">tibiotarsus</a> will automatically be pulled and tighten when the leg bends, causing the foot to curl and become stiff when the bird lands on a branch. This enables passerines to sleep while perching without falling off.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most passerine birds develop 12 tail feathers, although the <a href="/wiki/Superb_lyrebird" title="Superb lyrebird">superb lyrebird</a> has 16.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Certain species of passerines have stiff tail feathers, which help the birds balance themselves when perching upon vertical surfaces. Some passerines, specifically in the family <a href="/wiki/Ploceidae" title="Ploceidae">Ploceidae</a>, are well known for their elaborate sexual ornaments, including extremely long tails. A well-known example is the <a href="/wiki/Long-tailed_widowbird" title="Long-tailed widowbird">long-tailed widowbird</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Eggs_and_nests">Eggs and nests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Passerine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Eggs and nests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The chicks of passerines are <a href="/wiki/Altricial" class="mw-redirect" title="Altricial">altricial</a>: blind, featherless, and helpless when hatched from their eggs. Hence, the chicks require extensive parental care. Most passerines lay coloured eggs, in contrast with nonpasserines, most of whose eggs are white except in some ground-nesting groups such as <a href="/wiki/Charadriiformes" title="Charadriiformes">Charadriiformes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nightjar" title="Nightjar">nightjars</a>, where camouflage is necessary, and in some <a href="/wiki/Brood_parasite" title="Brood parasite">parasitic</a> <a href="/wiki/Cuckoo" title="Cuckoo">cuckoos</a>, which match the passerine host\'s egg. <a href="/wiki/Vinous-throated_parrotbill" title="Vinous-throated parrotbill">Vinous-throated parrotbill</a> has two egg colours, white and blue. This can prevent the brood parasitic <a href="/wiki/Common_cuckoo" title="Common cuckoo">Common cuckoo</a>.\n</p><p>Clutches vary considerably in size: some larger passerines of Australia such as lyrebirds and scrub-robins lay only a single egg, most smaller passerines in warmer climates lay between two and five, while in the higher latitudes of the Northern Hemisphere, hole-nesting species like <a href="/wiki/Tit_(bird)" title="Tit (bird)">tits</a> can lay up to a dozen and other species around five or six.\nThe family <a href="/wiki/Viduidae" title="Viduidae">Viduidae</a> do not build their own nests, instead, they lay eggs in other birds\' nests.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_and_evolution">Origin and evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Passerine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Origin and evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The evolutionary history of the passerine families and the relationships among them remained rather mysterious until the late 20th century. In many cases, passerine families were grouped together on the basis of morphological similarities that, it is now believed, are the result of <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a>, not a close genetic relationship. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Troglodytidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Troglodytidae">wrens of the Americas and Eurasia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Maluridae" class="mw-redirect" title="Maluridae">those of Australia</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Acanthisittidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Acanthisittidae">those of New Zealand</a> look superficially similar and behave in similar ways, and yet belong to three far-flung branches of the passerine family tree; they are as unrelated as it is possible to be while remaining Passeriformes.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Much research remains to be done, but advances in <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a> and improved <a href="/wiki/Paleobiogeography" class="mw-redirect" title="Paleobiogeography">paleobiogeographical</a> data gradually are revealing a clearer picture of passerine origins and evolution that reconciles molecular affinities, the constraints of morphology and the specifics of the fossil record.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Claramunt_&amp;_Cracraft_2015_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Claramunt_&amp;_Cracraft_2015-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The first passerines are now thought to have evolved in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a> in the late <a href="/wiki/Paleocene" title="Paleocene">Paleocene</a> or early <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a>, around 50 million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Claramunt_&amp;_Cracraft_2015_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Claramunt_&amp;_Cracraft_2015-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The initial split was between the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_wren" title="New Zealand wren">New Zealand wrens</a> (<a href="/wiki/Acanthisittidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Acanthisittidae">Acanthisittidae</a>) and all other passerines, and the second split involved the <a href="/wiki/Tyranni" title="Tyranni">Tyranni</a> (suboscines) and the <a href="/wiki/Passeri" class="mw-redirect" title="Passeri">Passeri</a> (oscines or songbirds). The latter experienced a great radiation of forms out of the <a href="/wiki/Australian_continent" class="mw-redirect" title="Australian continent">Australian continent</a>. A major branch of the Passeri, <a href="/wiki/Parvorder" class="mw-redirect" title="Parvorder">parvorder</a> <a href="/wiki/Passerida" title="Passerida">Passerida</a>, expanded deep into Eurasia and Africa, where a further explosive radiation of new lineages occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-Claramunt_&amp;_Cracraft_2015_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Claramunt_&amp;_Cracraft_2015-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> This eventually led to three major <a href="/wiki/Passerida" title="Passerida">Passerida</a> lineages comprising about 4,000 species, which in addition to the <a href="/wiki/Corvida" title="Corvida">Corvida</a> and numerous minor lineages make up songbird diversity today. Extensive <a href="/wiki/Biogeographical" class="mw-redirect" title="Biogeographical">biogeographical</a> mixing happens, with northern forms returning to the south, southern forms moving north, and so on.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_record">Fossil record</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Passerine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Fossil record">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Earliest_passerines">Earliest passerines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Passerine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Earliest passerines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Superb_lyrbird_in_scrub.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Superb_lyrbird_in_scrub.jpg/220px-Superb_lyrbird_in_scrub.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Superb_lyrbird_in_scrub.jpg/330px-Superb_lyrbird_in_scrub.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Superb_lyrbird_in_scrub.jpg/440px-Superb_lyrbird_in_scrub.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1067" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Superb_lyrbird_in_scrub.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Male <a href="/wiki/Superb_lyrebird" title="Superb lyrebird">superb lyrebird</a> (<i>Menura novaehollandiae</i>): This very primitive <a href="/wiki/Songbird" title="Songbird">songbird</a> shows strong <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a>, with a peculiarly <a href="/wiki/Apomorph" class="mw-redirect" title="Apomorph">apomorphic</a> display of plumage in males.</div></div></div>\n<p>Perching bird <a href="/wiki/Osteology" title="Osteology">osteology</a>, especially of the limb bones, is rather diagnostic.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> However, the early fossil record is poor because the first Passeriformes were apparently on the small side of the present size range, and their delicate bones did not preserve well. <a href="/wiki/Queensland_Museum" title="Queensland Museum">Queensland Museum</a> specimens F20688 (<a href="/wiki/Carpometacarpus" title="Carpometacarpus">carpometacarpus</a>) and F24685 (<a href="/wiki/Tibiotarsus" title="Tibiotarsus">tibiotarsus</a>) from <a href="/wiki/Murgon,_Queensland" class="mw-redirect" title="Murgon, Queensland">Murgon, Queensland</a>, are fossil bone fragments initially assigned to <a href="/wiki/Passeriformes" class="mw-redirect" title="Passeriformes">Passeriformes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> However, the material is too fragmentary and their affinities have been questioned.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Several more recent fossils from the <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a> of Europe that are more complete definitely represent early passeriforms, although their exact position in the evolutionary tree is not known.\n</p><p>From the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bathans_Formation&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bathans Formation (page does not exist)">Bathans Formation</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Manuherikia_River" title="Manuherikia River">Manuherikia River</a> in <a href="/wiki/Otago" title="Otago">Otago</a>, New Zealand, <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_New_Zealand_Te_Papa_Tongarewa" title="Museum of New Zealand Te Papa Tongarewa">MNZ</a> S42815 (a <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location#Proximal_and_distal" title="Anatomical terms of location">distal</a> right <a href="/wiki/Tarsometatarsus" title="Tarsometatarsus">tarsometatarsus</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Tui_(bird)" title="Tui (bird)">tui</a>-sized bird) and several bones of at least one species of <a href="/wiki/Saddleback_(bird)" title="Saddleback (bird)">saddleback</a>-sized bird have recently been described. These date from the Early to <a href="/wiki/Middle_Miocene" title="Middle Miocene">Middle Miocene</a> (<a href="/w/index.php?title=Awamoan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Awamoan (page does not exist)">Awamoan</a> to <a href="/wiki/Lillburnian" class="mw-redirect" title="Lillburnian">Lillburnian</a>, 19\xe2\x80\x9316&#160;mya).<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Modern knowledge about the living passerines\' interrelationships (see the <a href="#Taxonomic_list_of_Passeriformes_families">list of families</a> below) suggests that the last common ancestor of all living Passeriformes was a small forest bird, probably with a stubby tail<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> and an overall drab coloration, but possibly with marked sexual dimorphism. The latter trait seems to have been lost and re-evolved multiple times in songbird evolution alone, judging from its distribution among the extant lineages.\nSexual dichromatism is very rare among the <a href="/wiki/Basal_(evolution)" class="mw-redirect" title="Basal (evolution)">basal</a> lineages of Passerida, and probably their <a href="/wiki/Plesiomorph" class="mw-redirect" title="Plesiomorph">plesiomorphic</a> condition. But among the youngest passerid clade, the <a href="/wiki/Passeroidea" class="mw-redirect" title="Passeroidea">Passeroidea</a>, extremely colorful males and drab females are common, if not the rule. On the other hand, among the basalmost Passeri a considerable number of strongly dimorphic lineages exist, too, such as the very ancient <a href="/wiki/Menuridae" class="mw-redirect" title="Menuridae">Menuridae</a>, as well as many <a href="/wiki/Meliphagoidea" title="Meliphagoidea">Meliphagoidea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corvoidea" title="Corvoidea">Corvoidea</a>. Sexual dimorphism is also not uncommon in the <a href="/wiki/Acanthisittidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Acanthisittidae">Acanthisittidae</a> and prominent in some suboscines such as the <a href="/wiki/Pipridae" class="mw-redirect" title="Pipridae">Pipridae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cotingidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Cotingidae">Cotingidae</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_European_passerines">Early European passerines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Passerine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Early European passerines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wieslochia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Wieslochia.jpg/170px-Wieslochia.jpg" width="170" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Wieslochia.jpg/255px-Wieslochia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Wieslochia.jpg/340px-Wieslochia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="648" data-file-height="459" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wieslochia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Wieslochia</i> fossil</div></div></div> \n<p>In Europe, perching birds are not too uncommon in the fossil record from the <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a> onward, but most are too fragmentary for a more definite placement:\n</p>\n<ul><li><i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wieslochia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Wieslochia (page does not exist)">Wieslochia</a></i> (Early Oligocene of Frauenweiler, Germany)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Jamna_(bird)" title="Jamna (bird)">Jamna</a></i> (Early Oligocene of Jamna Dolna, Poland)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Resoviaornis" title="Resoviaornis">Resoviaornis</a></i> (Early Oligocene of Wola Rafa\xc5\x82owska, Poland)</li>\n<li>Passeriformes gen. et sp. indet. (Early Oligocene of Luberon, France) \xe2\x80\x93 suboscine or basal<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Passeriformes gen. et spp. indet. (Late Oligocene of France) \xe2\x80\x93 several suboscine and oscine taxa<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Passeriformes gen. et spp. indet. (Middle Miocene of France and Germany) \xe2\x80\x93 basal?<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Passeriformes gen. et spp. indet. (Saj\xc3\xb3v\xc3\xb6lgyi Middle Miocene of M\xc3\xa1trasz\xc5\x91l\xc5\x91s, Hungary) \xe2\x80\x93 at least 2 taxa, possibly 3; at least one probably Oscines<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Passeriformes gen. et sp. indet. (Middle Miocene of Fels\xc5\x91t\xc3\xa1rk\xc3\xa1ny, Hungary) \xe2\x80\x93 oscine?<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Passeriformes gen. et sp. indet. (Late Miocene of Polg\xc3\xa1rdi, Hungary) \xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/Sylvioidea" title="Sylvioidea">Sylvioidea</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sylviidae" title="Sylviidae">Sylviidae</a>? <a href="/wiki/Cettiidae" title="Cettiidae">Cettiidae</a>?)<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p><i>Wieslochia</i> was possibly not a member of any extant suborder. That not only the Passeri expanded much beyond their region of origin is proven by an undetermined <a href="/wiki/Broadbill" title="Broadbill">broadbill</a> (Eurylaimidae) from the Early <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> (roughly 20&#160;mya) of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Wintershof&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Wintershof (page does not exist)">Wintershof</a>, Germany, and the indeterminant Late Oligocene suboscine from France listed above. Even very <a href="/wiki/Basal_(evolution)" class="mw-redirect" title="Basal (evolution)">basal</a> Passeriformes might have been common in Europe until the Middle Miocene, some 12&#160;mya.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Extant Passeri super-families were quite distinct by that time and are known since about 12\xe2\x80\x9313 mya when modern genera were present in the corvoidean and basal songbirds. The modern diversity of Passerida genera is known mostly from the Late Miocene onwards and into the <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">Pliocene</a> (about 10\xe2\x80\x932&#160;mya). <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Lagerst%C3%A4tten" class="mw-redirect" title="Lagerst\xc3\xa4tten">lagerst\xc3\xa4tten</a> (&lt;1.8&#160;mya) yield numerous extant species, and many yield almost nothing but extant species or their <a href="/wiki/Chronospecies" title="Chronospecies">chronospecies</a> and paleosubspecies.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="American_fossils">American fossils</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Passerine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: American fossils">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Late_Quaternary_prehistoric_birds" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Quaternary prehistoric birds">Late Quaternary prehistoric birds</a></div>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, the fossil record is more scant before the Pleistocene, from which several still-existing suboscine families are documented. Apart from the indeterminable <a href="/w/index.php?title=MACN&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="MACN (page does not exist)">MACN</a>-SC-1411 (Pinturas Early/Middle Miocene of Santa Cruz Province, Argentina),<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> an extinct lineage of perching birds has been described from the Late Miocene of California, United States: the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Palaeoscinidae&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Palaeoscinidae (page does not exist)">Palaeoscinidae</a> with the single genus <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Paleoscinis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Paleoscinis (page does not exist)">Paleoscinis</a></i>. <i>"Palaeostruthus" eurius</i> (Pliocene of Florida) probably belongs to an extant family, most likely <a href="/wiki/Passeroidea" class="mw-redirect" title="Passeroidea">passeroidean</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Systematics_and_taxonomy">Systematics and taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Passerine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Systematics and taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Corvida" title="Corvida">Corvida</a> and Passerida were classified as parvorders in the suborder Passeri; in accord with the usual <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomic</a> practice, they would probably be ranked as <a href="/wiki/Infraorder" class="mw-redirect" title="Infraorder">infraorders</a>. As originally envisioned in the <a href="/wiki/Sibley-Ahlquist_taxonomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Sibley-Ahlquist taxonomy">Sibley-Ahlquist taxonomy</a>, they contained, respectively, the large <a href="/wiki/Taxonomic_rank" title="Taxonomic rank">superfamilies</a> Corvoidea and Meliphagoidea, as well as minor lineages, and the superfamilies Sylvioidea, Muscicapoidea, and Passeroidea.\n</p><p>The arrangement has been found to be oversimplified by more recent research. Since the mid-2000s, literally, dozens of studies are being published that try rather successfully to resolve the <a href="/wiki/Phylogeny" class="mw-redirect" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a> of the passeriform <a href="/wiki/Radiation_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Radiation (biology)">radiation</a>. For example, the Corvida in the traditional sense was a rather arbitrary assemblage of early and/or minor lineages of passeriform birds of Old World origin, generally from the region of Australia, New Zealand, and <a href="/wiki/Wallacea" title="Wallacea">Wallacea</a>. The Passeri, though, can be made monophyletic by moving some families about, but the "clean" three-superfamily-arrangement has turned out to be far more complex and it is uncertain whether future authors will stick to it.\n</p><p>Major "<a href="/wiki/Wastebin_taxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Wastebin taxon">wastebin</a>" families such as the <a href="/wiki/Old_World_warbler" title="Old World warbler">Old World warblers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Old_World_babbler" title="Old World babbler">Old World babblers</a> have turned out to be <a href="/wiki/Paraphyletic" class="mw-redirect" title="Paraphyletic">paraphyletic</a> and are being rearranged. Several taxa turned out to represent highly distinct species-poor lineages, so new families had to be established, some of them \xe2\x80\x93 like the <a href="/wiki/Stitchbird" title="Stitchbird">stitchbird</a> of New Zealand and the <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasian</a> <a href="/wiki/Bearded_reedling" title="Bearded reedling">bearded reedling</a> \xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/Monotypic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monotypic">monotypic</a> with only one living species.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In the Passeri alone, a number of minor lineages will eventually be recognized as distinct superfamilies. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Kinglet" title="Kinglet">kinglets</a> constitute a single genus with less than 10 species today but seem to have been among the first perching bird lineages to diverge as the group spread across Eurasia. No particularly close relatives of them have been found among comprehensive studies of the living Passeri, though they might be fairly close to some little-studied tropical Asian groups. Treatment of the <a href="/wiki/Nuthatch" title="Nuthatch">nuthatches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wren" title="Wren">wrens</a>, and their closest relatives as a distinct super-family <a href="/wiki/Certhioidea" title="Certhioidea">Certhioidea</a> is increasingly considered justified; the same might eventually apply to the tits and their closest relatives.\n</p><p>This process is still continuing. Therefore, the arrangement as presented here is subject to change. However, it should take precedence over unreferenced conflicting treatments in family, genus, and species articles here.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomic_list_of_Passeriformes_families">Taxonomic list of Passeriformes families</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Passerine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Taxonomic list of Passeriformes families">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rock_wren.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Rock_wren.jpg/220px-Rock_wren.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Rock_wren.jpg/330px-Rock_wren.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Rock_wren.jpg/440px-Rock_wren.jpg 2x" data-file-width="650" data-file-height="433" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rock_wren.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_rock_wren" title="New Zealand rock wren">New Zealand rock wren</a> (<i>Xenicus gilviventris</i>), one of the two surviving species of suborder <a href="/wiki/Acanthisitti" class="mw-redirect" title="Acanthisitti">Acanthisitti</a></div></div></div>\n<p>This list is in taxonomic order, placing related species/groups next to each other. The Passerida subdivisions are updated as needed from the default sequence of the <i><a href="/wiki/Handbook_of_the_Birds_of_the_World" title="Handbook of the Birds of the World">Handbook of the Birds of the World</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> based on the most modern and comprehensive studies.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrangement_of_families">Arrangement of families</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Passerine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Arrangement of families">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The families are sorted into a somewhat novel sequence unlike that in older works, where e.g. Corvidae are placed last. This is because so many reallocations have taken place since about 2005 that a definite <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomy</a> has not been established yet, although the <a href="/wiki/Phylogeny" class="mw-redirect" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a> is by and large resolved. The present sequence is an attempt to preserve as much of the traditional sequence while giving priority to adequately addressing the phylogenetic relationships between the families. Based on John Boyd\'s <i>Taxonomy in Flux Checklist 3.5</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Boyd_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyd-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" (January 2008)">verification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Menuridae" class="mw-redirect" title="Menuridae">Menuridae</a>: lyrebirds</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Atrichornithidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Atrichornithidae">Atrichornithidae</a>: scrub-birds</li></ul></li></ul>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Regentbowerbirdmale.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Regentbowerbirdmale.jpg/220px-Regentbowerbirdmale.jpg" width="220" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/63/Regentbowerbirdmale.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="303" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Regentbowerbirdmale.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Male <a href="/wiki/Regent_bowerbird" title="Regent bowerbird">regent bowerbird</a> (<i>Sericulus chrysocephalus</i>, <a href="/wiki/Ptilonorhynchidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Ptilonorhynchidae">Ptilonorhynchidae</a>)</div></div></div>\n<ul><li><b>Infraorder Climacterides</b> \xe2\x80\x93 Possible superfamily "Ptilonorhynchoidea"<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). bowerbirds</li></ul></li>\n<li><b>Infraorder Meliphagides</b> \xe2\x80\x93 mainly insectivores and nectarivores, distribution centered on Australo-Melanesian region extending into surroundings, white markings, males and females look alike\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Orthonychidae" title="Orthonychidae">Orthonychidae</a>: logrunners</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Pomatostomidae" class="mw-redirect" Australasian region and surroundings: The oldest truly globally successful group of passerines, they include among them what may well be the most intelligent and the most spectacular of the jewel-babblers</li></ul></li>\n<li>Orioloidea\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Eulacestomidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Eulacestomidae">Eulacestomidae</a>: Wattled ploughbills</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Hypocoliidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Hypocoliidae">Hypocoliidae</a>: hypocolius, tentatively placed here</li></ul></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Certhioidea" title="Certhioidea">Certhioidea</a> \xe2\x80\x93 wrens, treecreepers and allies. Sometimes included in Muscicapoidea. Occur in the Americas and Eurasia, though mostly north of the <a href="/wiki/Alpide_belt" title="Alpide belt">Alpide belt</a>. Distribution and the <a href="/wiki/Basal_(evolution)" class="mw-redirect" title="Basal (evolution)">basal</a> fossil <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Certhiops&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Certhiops (page does not exist)">Certhiops</a></i> indicates the Certhioidea probably originated around the end of the <a href="/wiki/Paleogene" title="Paleogene">Paleogene</a> somewhere around the North Atlantic. Small, often tiny, tend to be conspicuously plump but nimble; many accomplished climbers. Plumage extremely <a href="/wiki/Crypsis" title="Crypsis">cryptic</a> or somewhat colorful, in the latter case typically blue-grey at least on the upperside. Sexes look identical or almost so, but may differ strongly in vocalizations.\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Tichodromadidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Tichodromadidae">Tichodromadidae</a>: wallcreeper: Traditionally placed as a subfamily of the nuthatches and more rarely of the treecreepers, no study has been able to verify either placement this far. Thus it is better considered a monotypic family, at least for the time being.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Sittidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Sittidae">Sittidae</a>: nuthatches</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Certhiidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Certhiidae">Certhiidae</a>: treecreepers</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Salpornithidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Salpornithidae">Salpornithidae</a>: spotted creepers. Tentatively placed here; often considered a subfamily of the Certhidae.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Troglodytidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Troglodytidae">Troglodytidae</a>: wrens</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Polioptilidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Polioptilidae">Polioptilidae</a>: gnatcatchers</li></ul></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Muscicapoidea" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscicapoidea">Muscicapoidea</a> \xe2\x80\x93 "Old World flycatchers", starlings and allies. Mostly insectivores, near-global distribution centered on Old World <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a>. One family <a href="/wiki/Endemic_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Endemic (biology)">endemic</a> to Americas. Nearly absent (except introductions) from the Australian region. Usually rather robust for their size, some species (especially of Cinclidae, Buphagidae and Mimidae) are quite dark and dull while others are commonly colorful or/and iridescent. Sexual dimorphism often absent, sometimes pronounced.\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cinclidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Cinclidae">Cinclidae</a>: dippers, the most aquatic members of the whole clade.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Turdidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Turdidae">Turdidae</a>: thrushes and allies. Monophyly needs confirmation.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Muscicapidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscicapidae">Muscicapidae</a>: Old World flycatchers and chats. Monophyly needs confirmation.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Buphagidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Buphagidae">Buphagidae</a>: oxpeckers. Formerly usually included in Sturnidae.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Sturnidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Sturnidae">Sturnidae</a>: starlings and possibly <a href="/wiki/Philippine_creeper" title="Philippine creeper">Philippine creepers</a>. Placement of latter in Muscicapoidea seems good, but inclusion in Sturnidae requires confirmation; possibly distinct family Rhabdornithidae.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Mimidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Mimidae">Mimidae</a>: mockingbirds and thrashers <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pinz%C3%B3n_azul_de_Gran_Canaria_(macho),_M._A._Pe%C3%B1a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Pinz%C3%B3n_azul_de_Gran_Canaria_%28macho%29%2C_M._A._Pe%C3%B1a.jpg/220px-Pinz%C3%B3n_azul_de_Gran_Canaria_%28macho%29%2C_M._A._Pe%C3%B1a.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Pinz%C3%B3n_azul_de_Gran_Canaria_%28macho%29%2C_M._A._Pe%C3%B1a.jpg/330px-Pinz%C3%B3n_azul_de_Gran_Canaria_%28macho%29%2C_M._A._Pe%C3%B1a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Pinz%C3%B3n_azul_de_Gran_Canaria_%28macho%29%2C_M._A._Pe%C3%B1a.jpg/440px-Pinz%C3%B3n_azul_de_Gran_Canaria_%28macho%29%2C_M._A._Pe%C3%B1a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="798" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pinz%C3%B3n_azul_de_Gran_Canaria_(macho),_M._A._Pe%C3%B1a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gran_Canaria_blue_chaffinch" title="Gran Canaria blue chaffinch">Gran Canaria blue chaffinch</a> (male).</div></div></div></li></ul></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Passeroidea" class="mw-redirect" title="Passeroidea">Passeroidea</a> \xe2\x80\x93 "finches", "New World warblers/blackbirds" and allies. Mostly herbivores including many seed-eaters but some specialized insectivores; near-global distribution centered on <a href="/wiki/Palearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palearctic">Palearctic</a> and Americas. Includes the <a href="/wiki/Nine-primaried_oscine" title="Nine-primaried oscine">nine-primaried oscines</a> (probably a subclade). A very high proportion of colorful and highly sexually dimorphic forms.\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Promeropidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Promeropidae">Promeropidae</a> (Sugarbirds), living fossils with a nondescript "<a href="/wiki/Honeyeater" title="Honeyeater">honeyeater</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Warbler" title="Warbler">warbler</a>" phenotype, a <i><a href="/wiki/Capensis" class="mw-redirect" title="Capensis">Capensis</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Relict_(biology)" title="Relict (biology)">relict</a> lineage only found in Africa south of the Equatorial region, Include keystone species for <a href="/wiki/Fynbos" title="Fynbos">fynbos</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Afromontane_forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Afromontane forest">Afromontane forest</a> ecosystems, sexual dimorphism does not appear pronounced, but includes many little-studied species</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Arcanatoridae" class="mw-redirect" title="Arcanatoridae">Arcanatoridae</a> (Dapplethroat &amp; allies)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Dicaeidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Dicaeidae">Dicaeidae</a> (flowerpeckers), sunbirds and flowerpeckers, might be included in Passeroidea, eat mostly nectar, pollen, and berries, supplemented with arthropods, generally restricted to the Old World tropics, plumage usually extremely colorful at least in males, usually a few contrasting hues (commonly including black and/or white, and usually including red) and in some with metallic sheen, sexual dimorphism usually pronounced, females in most species greenish above, lighter below</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Nectariniidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Nectariniidae">Nectariniidae</a>: sunbirds</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Chloropseidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Chloropseidae">Chloropseidae</a>: leafbirds</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Irenidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Irenidae">Irenidae</a>: fairy-bluebirds.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Urocynchramidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Urocynchramidae">Urocynchramidae</a>: Przewalski\'s finch. Recently split from Fringillidae; tentatively placed here.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Peucedramidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Peucedramidae">Peucedramidae</a>: olive warbler</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Prunellidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Prunellidae">Prunellidae</a>: accentors</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ploceidae" title="Ploceidae">Ploceidae</a>: weavers. Certain members of <i>Ploceidae</i>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Long-tailed_widowbird" title="Long-tailed widowbird">long-tailed widowbird</a> are well known for their elaborate sexual ornaments.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Viduidae" title="Viduidae">Viduidae</a>: indigobirds and whydahs</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Estrildidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Estrildidae">Estrildidae</a>: estrildid finches (waxbills, munias and allies)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Passeridae" class="mw-redirect" title="Passeridae">Passeridae</a> true sparrows</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Motacillidae" title="Motacillidae">Motacillidae</a>: wagtails and pipits</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Nine-primaried_oscine" title="Nine-primaried oscine">Nine-primaried oscines</a>:\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Fringillidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Fringillidae">Fringillidae</a>: true finches and <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_honeycreeper" title="Hawaiian honeycreeper">Hawaiian honeycreepers</a>. Possibly polyphyletic.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Calcariidae" title="Calcariidae">Calcariidae</a>: longspurs and snow buntings</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Emberizidae" title="Emberizidae">Emberizidae</a>: buntings</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Passerellidae" title="Passerellidae">Passerellidae</a>: American sparrows</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Icteridae" class="mw-redirect" title="Icteridae">Icteridae</a>: grackles, New World blackbirds, and New World orioles</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Parulidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Parulidae">Parulidae</a>: New World warblers</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Thraupidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Thraupidae">Thraupidae</a>: tanagers and allies</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Cardinalidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardinalidae">Cardinalidae</a>: cardinals</li></ul></li></ul></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Passerida" title="Passerida">Passerida</a> <i>incertae sedis</i>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Scotocercidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Scotocercidae">Scotocercidae</a>: streaked scrub warbler</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Rhagologidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhagologidae">Rhagologidae</a>: mottled whistler</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Prionopidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Prionopidae">Prionopidae</a>: helmetshrikes and woodshrikes</li></ul></li></ul></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny">Phylogeny</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Passerine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Phylogeny">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Living Passeriformes based on the "Taxonomy in Flux family phylogenetic tree" by John Boyd.<sup id="cite_ref-Boyd_28-1"
